<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Potato</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Plant species producing the tuber used as a staple food</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Potato_(disambiguation)" title="Potato (disambiguation)">Potato (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center"><b><span class="binomial"><span style="font-weight:normal;"></span><i>Solanum tuberosum</i></span></b><br/><div style="font-size: 85%;"><a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">L.</a></div>

<p>The <b>potato</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Starch#Food" title="Starch">starchy</a> <a href="/wiki/Tuber" title="Tuber">tuber</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> <i><b>Solanum tuberosum</b></i> and is a <a href="/wiki/Root_vegetable" title="Root vegetable">root vegetable</a> and a fruit native to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. The plant is a <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> in the nightshade family <a href="/wiki/Solanaceae" title="Solanaceae">Solanaceae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wild potato <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> can be found from the southern United States to southern Chile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The potato was originally believed to have been domesticated by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> independently in multiple locations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UniWisconsin_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-UniWisconsin-4">[4]</a></sup> but later genetic studies traced a single origin, in the area of present-day southern Peru and extreme northwestern <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>. Potatoes were <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> there approximately 7,000–10,000 years ago, from a species in the <i><a href="/wiki/Solanum_brevicaule" title="Solanum brevicaule">Solanum brevicaule</a></i> complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spooner_2005_14694–99_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spooner_2005_14694–99-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LostCrops_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-LostCrops-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Michael_Francis_2005_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Michael_Francis_2005-7">[7]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> region of South America, where the species is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_species" title="Indigenous species">indigenous</a>, some close relatives of the potato are cultivated.
</p><p>Potatoes were introduced to Europe from the Americas by the Spanish in the second half of the 16th century. Today they are a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> in many parts of the world and an integral part of much of the world's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_supply" title="Food supply">food supply</a>. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Potato&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, potatoes were the world's fourth-largest food crop after <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (corn), <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potpro_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-potpro-8">[8]</a></sup> Following millennia of <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>, there are now over 5,000 <a href="/wiki/List_of_potato_cultivars" title="List of potato cultivars">different types of potatoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LostCrops_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-LostCrops-6">[6]</a></sup> Over 99% of potatoes presently cultivated worldwide descend from varieties that originated in the lowlands of <a href="/wiki/Zona_Sur" title="Zona Sur">south-central Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ames2008_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ames2008-9">[9]</a></sup> The importance of the potato as a food source and culinary ingredient varies by region and is still changing. It remains an essential crop in Europe, especially Northern and Eastern Europe, where <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> production is still the highest in the world, while the most rapid expansion in production since 2000 has occurred in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">southern</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Asia" title="Eastern Asia">eastern Asia</a>, with China and India leading the world in overall production as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Potato&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.
</p><p>Like the <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, the potato is a nightshade in the genus <i>Solanum</i>, and the vegetative and fruiting parts of the potato contain the toxin <a href="/wiki/Solanine" title="Solanine">solanine</a> which is dangerous for human consumption. Normal potato tubers that have been grown and stored properly produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycoalkaloids" title="Glycoalkaloids">glycoalkaloids</a> in amounts small enough to be negligible to human health, but, if green sections of the plant (namely sprouts and skins) are exposed to light, the tuber can accumulate a high enough concentration of glycoalkaloids to affect human health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fried_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-fried-10">[10]</a></sup> The discovery of acrylamides in starchy foods in 2002 led to international health concerns, but subsequent high-quality evidence showed acrylamide is not likely to cause cancer in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cruk_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-cruk-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-acs-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Spud" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Spud_(disambiguation)" title="Spud (disambiguation)">Spud (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p>The English word <i>potato</i> comes from Spanish <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">patata</i></span> (the name used in Spain). The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Spanish_Academy" title="Royal Spanish Academy">Royal Spanish Academy</a> says the Spanish word is a hybrid of the <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_language" title="Taíno language">Taíno</a> <span title="Taino-language text"><i lang="tnq">batata</i></span> ('<a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a>') and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" title="Quechua languages">Quechua</a> <span title="Quechua-language text"><i lang="qu">papa</i></span> ('potato').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley196804_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ley196804-14">[14]</a></sup> The name originally referred to the <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a> although the two plants are not closely related. The 16th-century English herbalist <a href="/wiki/John_Gerard" title="John Gerard">John Gerard</a> referred to sweet potatoes as <i>common potatoes</i>, and used the terms <i>bastard potatoes</i> and <i>Virginia potatoes</i> for the species now known as potato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-15">[15]</a></sup> In many of the chronicles detailing agriculture and plants, no distinction is made between the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Potatoes are occasionally referred to as <i>Irish potatoes</i> or <i>white potatoes</i> in the United States, to distinguish them from sweet potatoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <i>spud</i> for a potato comes from the digging of soil (or a hole) prior to the planting of potatoes. The word has an unknown origin and was originally (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1440</span>) used as a term for a short knife or dagger, probably related to the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">spad-</i></span>, a word root meaning "sword"; compare Spanish <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">espada</i></span>, English "spade", and <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/spadroon" title="wikt:spadroon">spadroon</a></i>. It subsequently transferred over to a variety of digging tools. Around 1845, the name transferred to the tuber itself, the first record of this usage being in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand English</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The origin of the word <i>spud</i> has erroneously been attributed to an 18th-century activist group dedicated to keeping the potato out of Britain, calling itself the Society for the Prevention of Unwholesome Diet. It was <a href="/wiki/Mario_Pei" title="Mario Pei">Mario Pei</a>'s 1949 <i>The Story of Language</i> that can be blamed for the word's <a href="/wiki/False_etymology" title="False etymology">false origin</a>. Pei wrote "the potato, for its part, was in disrepute some centuries ago. Some Englishmen who did not fancy potatoes formed a Society for the Prevention of Unwholesome Diet. The initials of the main words in this title gave rise to spud." Like many other claimed pre-20th century <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronymic</a> origins, this is false, and there is no evidence that a Society for the Prevention of Unwholesome Diet ever existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilton94_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilton94-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley196804_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ley196804-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>At least seven languages—Afrikaans, Dutch, French, (West) Frisian, Hebrew, Persian and some variants of German—are known to use a term for "potato" that translates roughly (or literally) into English as "earth apple" or "ground apple".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>

</li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_flowers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1821" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Potato_flowers.jpg/220px-Potato_flowers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Potato_flowers.jpg/330px-Potato_flowers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Potato_flowers.jpg/440px-Potato_flowers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_flowers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flowers of a potato plant</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_plants.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Potato_plants.jpg/220px-Potato_plants.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Potato_plants.jpg/330px-Potato_plants.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Potato_plants.jpg/440px-Potato_plants.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_plants.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potato plants</div></div></div>
<p>Potato plants are herbaceous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennials</a> that grow about 60 cm (24 in) high, depending on variety, with the leaves <a href="/wiki/Plant_senescence" title="Plant senescence">dying back</a> after flowering, fruiting and tuber formation. They bear white, pink, red, blue, or purple <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> with yellow <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">stamens</a>. Potatoes are mostly <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">cross-pollinated</a> by insects such as <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee" title="Bumblebee">bumblebees</a>, which carry pollen from other potato plants, though a substantial amount of self-fertilizing occurs as well. Tubers form in response to decreasing day length, although this tendency has been minimized in commercial varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>After flowering, potato plants produce small green <a href="/wiki/Potato_fruit" title="Potato fruit">fruits</a> that resemble green <a href="/wiki/Cherry_tomato" title="Cherry tomato">cherry tomatoes</a>, each containing about 300 <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a>. Like all parts of the plant except the tubers, the fruit contain the toxic <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloid</a> <a href="/wiki/Solanine" title="Solanine">solanine</a> and are therefore unsuitable for consumption. All new potato varieties are grown from seeds, also called "true potato seed", "TPS" or "botanical seed" to distinguish it from seed tubers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggers_2021_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggers_2021-22">[22]</a></sup> New varieties grown from seed can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetative_propagation" title="Vegetative propagation">propagated vegetatively</a> by planting tubers, pieces of tubers cut to include at least one or two eyes, or cuttings, a practice used in greenhouses for the production of healthy seed tubers. Plants propagated from tubers are clones of the parent, whereas those propagated from seed produce a range of different varieties.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Garden_potatoes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1302" data-file-width="3061" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Garden_potatoes.jpg/220px-Garden_potatoes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Garden_potatoes.jpg/330px-Garden_potatoes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Garden_potatoes.jpg/440px-Garden_potatoes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Garden_potatoes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potatoes in an Oklahoma garden</div></div></div>
<p>Potatoes, both <i>S. tuberosum</i> and most of its wild relatives, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-incompatible" title="Self-incompatible">self-incompatible</a>: they bear no useful fruit when self-pollinated. This trait is problematic for crop breeding, as all sexually-produced plants must be hybrids. The gene responsible for its trait as well as mutations to disable it are now known. Self-compatibility has successfully been introduced both to diploid potatoes (including a special line of <i>S. tuberosum</i>) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CRISPR-Cas9" title="CRISPR-Cas9">CRISPR-Cas9</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Plants having a 'Sli' gene produce pollen which is compatible to its own parent and plants with similar S genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hosaka_Hanneman,_Jr._1998_pp._191–197_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hosaka_Hanneman,_Jr._1998_pp._191–197-24">[24]</a></sup> This gene was recently cloned by <a href="/wiki/Wageningen_University_%26_Research" title="Wageningen University &amp; Research">Wageningen University</a> and Solynta in 2021, which would allow for faster and more focused breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggers_2021_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eggers_2021-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ma_Zhang_Zhang_Tang_p._25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ma_Zhang_Zhang_Tang_p.-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">Diploid</a> hybrid potato breeding is a recent area of potato genetics supported by the finding that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homozygous" title="Homozygous">homozygous</a> <a href="/wiki/Fixation_(population_genetics)" title="Fixation (population genetics)">fixation</a> of donor alleles is possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindhout_Meijer_Schotte_Hutten_2011_pp._301–312_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindhout_Meijer_Schotte_Hutten_2011_pp._301–312-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<p>There are about 5,000 potato varieties worldwide. Three thousand of them are found in the Andes alone, mainly in Peru, Bolivia, Ecuador, Chile, and Colombia. They belong to eight or nine species, depending on the taxonomic school. Apart from the 5,000 cultivated varieties, there are about 200 wild species and subspecies, many of which can be cross-bred with cultivated varieties. Cross-breeding has been done repeatedly to transfer resistances to certain pests and diseases from the gene pool of wild species to the gene pool of cultivated potato species.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2211" data-file-width="4378" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg/220px-Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg/330px-Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg/440px-Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russet_potato_cultivar_with_sprouts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russet potatoes</div></div></div>
<p>The major species grown worldwide is <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraploid" title="Tetraploid">tetraploid</a> with 48 <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>), and modern varieties of this species are the most widely cultivated. There are also four diploid species (with 24 chromosomes): <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>stenotomum</i>, <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>phureja</i>, <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>goniocalyx</i>, and <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>ajanhuiri</i>. There are two triploid species (with 36 chromosomes): <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>chaucha</i> and <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>juzepczukii</i>. There is one pentaploid cultivated species (with 60 chromosomes): <i>S.<span class="nowrap"> </span>curtilobum</i>. There are two major subspecies of <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>: <i>andigena</i>, or Andean; and <i>tuberosum</i>, or Chilean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Andean potato is adapted to the short-day conditions prevalent in the mountainous equatorial and tropical regions where it originated; the <a href="/wiki/Potatoes_of_Chilo%C3%A9" title="Potatoes of Chiloé">Chilean potato</a>, however, native to the <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Archipelago" title="Chiloé Archipelago">Chiloé Archipelago</a>, is adapted to the long-day conditions prevalent in the higher latitude region of southern Chile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodríguez_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rodríguez-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Potato_Center" title="International Potato Center">International Potato Center</a>, based in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lima,_Peru" title="Lima, Peru">Lima, Peru</a>, holds 4,870 types of potato <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">germplasm</a>, most of which are traditional <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">landrace</a> cultivars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The international Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium announced in 2009 that they had achieved a draft sequence of the potato genome, containing 12 chromosomes and 860 million base pairs, making it a medium-sized plant genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> More than 99 percent of all current <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a> of potatoes currently grown are direct descendants of a subspecies that once grew in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lowland" title="Lowland">lowlands</a> of south-central <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sd_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-sd-31">[31]</a></sup> Nonetheless, genetic testing of the wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> and wild species affirms that all potato subspecies derive from a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_(ecology)" title="Indigenous (ecology)">origin</a> in the area of present-day southern <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and extreme Northwestern <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> (from a species in the <i>Solanum brevicaule</i> complex).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spooner_2005_14694–99_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spooner_2005_14694–99-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LostCrops_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-LostCrops-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Michael_Francis_2005_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-John_Michael_Francis_2005-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most modern potatoes grown in North America arrived through European settlement and not independently from the South American sources, although at least one wild potato species, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Solanum_fendleri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Solanum fendleri (page does not exist)">Solanum fendleri</a></i>, naturally ranges from Peru into Texas, where it is used in breeding for resistance to a <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> species that attacks cultivated potatoes. A secondary center of genetic variability of the potato is Mexico, where important wild species that have been used extensively in modern breeding are found, such as the hexaploid <i><a href="/wiki/Solanum_demissum" title="Solanum demissum">Solanum demissum</a></i>, as a source of resistance to the devastating <a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">late blight disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlDis2011_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlDis2011-32">[32]</a></sup> Another relative native to this region, <i><a href="/wiki/Solanum_bulbocastanum" title="Solanum bulbocastanum">Solanum bulbocastanum</a></i>, has been used to genetically engineer the potato to resist potato blight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_potato_cultivars" title="List of potato cultivars">List of potato cultivars</a></div>
<p>There are close to 4,000 varieties of potatoes, each of which has specific agricultural or culinary attributes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Around 80 varieties are commercially available in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In general, varieties are categorized into a few main groups based on common characteristics, such as russet potatoes (rough brown skin), red potatoes, white potatoes, yellow potatoes (also called Yukon potatoes) and purple potatoes.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1089" data-file-width="922" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg/220px-Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg/330px-Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg/440px-Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_cells_dyed_for_starch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A thin section of a potato under light microscopy. It has been treated with an iodine based dye that binds to starch, turning it purple, showing the high starch content.</div></div></div>
<p>For culinary purposes, varieties are often differentiated by their waxiness: floury or mealy <i>baking</i> potatoes have more <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> (20–22%) than waxy <i>boiling</i> potatoes (16–18%). The distinction may also arise from variation in the comparative ratio of two different potato starch compounds: <a href="/wiki/Amylose" title="Amylose">amylose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>. Amylose, a long-chain molecule, diffuses from the starch granule when cooked in water, and lends itself to dishes where the potato is mashed. Varieties that contain a slightly higher amylopectin content, which is a highly branched molecule, help the potato retain its shape after being boiled in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Potatoes that are good for making <a href="/wiki/Potato_chip" title="Potato chip">potato chips</a> or potato crisps are sometimes called "chipping potatoes", which means they meet the basic requirements of similar varietal characteristics, being firm, fairly clean, and fairly well-shaped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immature potatoes may be sold fresh from the field as "creamer" or "new" potatoes<span class="anchor" id="creamer_potatoes"></span><span class="anchor" id="new_potatoes"></span> and are particularly valued for their taste. They are typically small in size and tender, with a loose skin, and flesh containing a lower level of <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> than other potatoes. In the United States they are generally either a <a href="/wiki/Yukon_Gold_potato" title="Yukon Gold potato">Yukon Gold potato</a> or a red potato, called gold creamers or red creamers respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recipe_tips_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-recipe_tips-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In the UK, the <a href="/wiki/Jersey_Royal" title="Jersey Royal">Jersey Royal</a> is a famous type of new potato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> They are distinct from "baby", "salad" or "<a href="/wiki/Fingerling_potato" title="Fingerling potato">fingerling</a>" potatoes, which are small and tend to have waxy flesh, but are grown to maturity and can be stored for months before being sold.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Cultivated_Potato_Database" title="European Cultivated Potato Database">European Cultivated Potato Database</a> (ECPD) is an online collaborative database of potato variety descriptions that is updated and maintained by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Agricultural_Science_Agency" title="Scottish Agricultural Science Agency">Scottish Agricultural Science Agency</a> within the framework of the European Cooperative Programme for Crop Genetic Resources Networks (ECP/GR)—which is run by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Plant_Genetic_Resources_Institute" title="International Plant Genetic Resources Institute">International Plant Genetic Resources Institute</a> (IPGRI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigmentation">Pigmentation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg/220px-Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg/330px-Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg/440px-Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Papas_de_colores_de_Chiloe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potatoes with different pigmentation</div></div></div>
<p>Dozens of potato <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">selectively bred</a> specifically for their skin or, more commonly, flesh <a href="/wiki/Biological_pigment" title="Biological pigment">color</a>, including gold, red, and blue varieties<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> that contain varying amounts of <a href="/wiki/Phytochemical" title="Phytochemical">phytochemicals</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carotenoids" title="Carotenoids">carotenoids</a> for gold/yellow or <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a> for red or blue cultivars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirsch_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirsch-43">[43]</a></sup> Carotenoid compounds include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Provitamin_A" title="Provitamin A">provitamin A</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-carotene" title="Alpha-carotene">alpha-carotene</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-carotene" title="Beta-carotene">beta-carotene</a>, which are converted to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrient</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>, during digestion. <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">Anthocyanins</a> mainly responsible for red or blue pigmentation in potato cultivars do not have nutritional significance, but are used for visual variety and consumer appeal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 2010, potatoes were <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">bioengineered</a> specifically for these pigmentation traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetically_engineered_potatoes">Genetically engineered potatoes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_engineered_potato" title="Genetically engineered potato">Genetically engineered potato</a></div>
<p>Genetic research has produced several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">genetically modified</a> varieties. 'New Leaf', owned by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monsanto_Company" title="Monsanto Company">Monsanto Company</a>, incorporates genes from <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bacillus thuringiensis</a></i>, which confers resistance to the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_potato_beetle" title="Colorado potato beetle">Colorado potato beetle</a>; 'New Leaf Plus' and 'New Leaf Y', approved by US regulatory agencies during the 1990s, also include resistance to viruses. <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burger_King" title="Burger King">Burger King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frito-Lay" title="Frito-Lay">Frito-Lay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Procter_%26_Gamble" title="Procter &amp; Gamble">Procter &amp; Gamble</a> announced they would not use genetically modified potatoes, and Monsanto published its intent to discontinue the line in March 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Waxy_potato_starch" title="Waxy potato starch">Waxy potato varieties</a> produce two main kinds of potato starch, <a href="/wiki/Amylose" title="Amylose">amylose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>, the latter of which is most industrially useful. <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">BASF</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Amflora" title="Amflora">Amflora</a> potato, which was modified to express <a href="/wiki/Antisense_RNA" title="Antisense RNA">antisense RNA</a> to inactivate the gene for <a href="/wiki/NDP-glucose%E2%80%94starch_glucosyltransferase" title="NDP-glucose—starch glucosyltransferase">granule bound starch synthase</a>, an enzyme which catalyzes the formation of amylose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Amflora potatoes therefore produce starch consisting almost entirely of <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>, and are thus more useful for the starch industry. In 2010, the European Commission cleared the way for 'Amflora' to be grown in the European Union for industrial purposes only—not for food. Nevertheless, under EU rules, individual countries have the right to decide whether they will allow this potato to be grown on their territory. Commercial planting of 'Amflora' was expected in the Czech Republic and Germany in the spring of 2010, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and the Netherlands in subsequent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Another GM potato variety developed by BASF is 'Fortuna' which was made resistant to <a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">late blight</a> by adding two resistance genes, blb1 and blb2, which originate from the Mexican wild potato Solanum bulbocastanum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In October 2011 BASF requested cultivation and marketing approval as a feed and food from the EFSA. In 2012, GMO development in Europe was stopped by BASF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In November 2014, the USDA approved a genetically modified potato developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.R._Simplot_Company" title="J.R. Simplot Company">J.R. Simplot Company</a>, which contains genetic modifications that prevent bruising and produce less <a href="/wiki/Acrylamide" title="Acrylamide">acrylamide</a> when fried than conventional potatoes; the modifications do not cause new proteins to be made, but rather prevent proteins from being made via <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genetically modified varieties have met public resistance in the United States and in the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosynthesis_of_starch">Biosynthesis of starch</span></h3>
<p>Sucrose is a product of photosynthesis. Ferreira <i>et al.</i> (2010) found that the genes for starch <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a> start to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_transcription" title="Gene transcription">transcribed</a> at the same time as <a href="/wiki/Sucrose_synthase" title="Sucrose synthase">sucrose synthase</a> activity begins. This transcription – including <a href="/wiki/Starch_synthase" title="Starch synthase">starch synthase</a> – also shows a <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> rhythm, correlating with the sucrose supply arriving from the leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zierer-et-al-2021_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zierer-et-al-2021-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_potato" title="History of the potato">History of the potato</a></div>
<p>The potato was first domesticated in the region of modern-day southern <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spooner_2005_14694–99_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spooner_2005_14694–99-5">[5]</a></sup> by pre-Columbian farmers, around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LostCrops_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-LostCrops-6">[6]</a></sup> It has since spread around the world and become a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple crop</a> in many countries.
</p><p>The earliest archaeologically verified potato tuber remains have been found at the coastal site of <a href="/wiki/Ancon_(archaeological_site)" title="Ancon (archaeological site)">Ancon</a> (central <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>), dating to 2500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The most widely cultivated variety, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solanum_tuberosum_tuberosum" title="Solanum tuberosum tuberosum">Solanum tuberosum tuberosum</a></i>, is indigenous to the <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Archipelago" title="Chiloé Archipelago">Chiloé Archipelago</a>, and has been cultivated by the local <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Chile" title="Indigenous peoples in Chile">indigenous people</a> since before the <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Chile" title="Conquest of Chile">Spanish conquest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodríguez_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rodríguez-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to conservative estimates, the introduction of the potato was responsible for a quarter of the growth in <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> population and urbanization between 1700 and 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In the Altiplano, potatoes provided the principal energy source for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_civilization" title="Inca civilization">Inca civilization</a>, its predecessors, and its Spanish successor. Following the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire">Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire</a>, the Spanish introduced the potato to Europe in the second half of the 16th century, part of the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>. The staple was subsequently conveyed by European (possibly including <a href="/wiki/Russian-American_Company" title="Russian-American Company">Russian</a>) mariners to territories and ports throughout the world, especially their colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sauer-2017_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sauer-2017-61">[61]</a></sup> The potato was slow to be adopted by European and colonial farmers, but after 1750 it became an important food staple and field crop<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sauer-2017_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sauer-2017-61">[61]</a></sup> and played a major role in the European 19th century population boom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Michael_Francis_2005_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-John_Michael_Francis_2005-7">[7]</a></sup> However, lack of genetic diversity, due to the very limited number of varieties initially introduced, left the crop vulnerable to disease. In 1845, a plant disease known as late blight, caused by the fungus-like <a href="/wiki/Oomycete" title="Oomycete">oomycete</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">Phytophthora infestans</a></i>, spread rapidly through the poorer communities of western <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> as well as parts of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a>, resulting in the crop failures that led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Irish_Famine" title="Great Irish Famine">Great Irish Famine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlDis2011_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-PlDis2011-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sauer-2017_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sauer-2017-61">[61]</a></sup> Thousands of varieties still persist in the Andes however, where over 100 cultivars might be found in a single valley, and a dozen or more might be maintained by a single agricultural household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Production_of_potatoes_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="309" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Production_of_potatoes_%282019%29.svg/220px-Production_of_potatoes_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Production_of_potatoes_%282019%29.svg/330px-Production_of_potatoes_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Production_of_potatoes_%282019%29.svg/440px-Production_of_potatoes_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Production_of_potatoes_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of potatoes (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PotatoYield.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/PotatoYield.png/220px-PotatoYield.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/PotatoYield.png/330px-PotatoYield.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/PotatoYield.png/440px-PotatoYield.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PotatoYield.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global production of potatoes in 2008</div></div></div>
<p>In 2020, world production of potatoes was 359 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by China with 22% of the total (table). Other major producers were India, Russia, Ukraine and the United States. It remains an essential crop in Europe (especially northern and eastern Europe), where per capita production is still the highest in the world, but the most rapid expansion over the past few decades has occurred in southern and eastern Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potpro_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-potpro-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao20_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-fao20-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h2>
<p>According to the United States Department of Agriculture, a typical raw potato is 79% water, 17% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> (88% is <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>), 2% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (see table). In a <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>100-gram (<span class="frac" role="math">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> oz) portion, raw potato provides 322 kilojoules (77 kilocalories) of food energy and is a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B6</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> (23% and 24% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>, respectively), with no other vitamins or minerals in significant amount (see table). The potato is rarely eaten raw because raw potato starch is poorly digested by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> When a potato is baked, its contents of vitamin B6 and vitamin C decline notably, while there is little significant change in the amount of other nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Potatoes are often broadly classified as having a high <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_index" title="Glycemic index">glycemic index</a> (GI) and so are often excluded from the diets of individuals trying to follow a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-glycemic_index_diet" title="Low-glycemic index diet">low-GI diet</a>. The GI of potatoes can vary considerably depending on the cultivar, growing conditions and storage, preparation methods (by cooking method, whether it is eaten hot or cold, whether it is mashed or cubed or consumed whole), and accompanying foods consumed (especially the addition of various high-fat or high-protein toppings).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gi_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-gi-67">[67]</a></sup> Consuming reheated or pre-cooked and cooled potatoes may yield a lower GI effect due to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Resistant_starch" title="Resistant starch">resistant starch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gi_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-gi-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the UK, potatoes are not considered by the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> (NHS) as counting or contributing towards the recommended daily <a href="/wiki/5_A_Day" title="5 A Day">five portions of fruit and vegetables</a>, the 5-A-Day program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_other_staple_foods">Comparison to other staple foods</span></h3>
<p>This table shows the nutrient content of potatoes next to other major staple foods, each one measured in its respective raw state on a <a href="/wiki/Dry_matter#Dry_matter_basis" title="Dry matter">dry weight basis</a> to account for their different water contents, even though staple foods are not commonly eaten raw and are usually sprouted or cooked before eating. In sprouted and cooked form, the relative nutritional and anti-nutritional contents of each of these grains (or other foods) may be different from the values in this table. Each nutrient (every row) has the highest number highlighted to show the staple food with the greatest amount in a dry 100 grams (3.5 oz) portion.
</p>

<p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1041539562">.mw-parser-output .citation{word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}</style><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_reference_name_AA"><a href="#ref_reference_name_AA"><b><sup>A</sup></b></a></span>  raw yellow dent corn<br/>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_reference_name_BB"><a href="#ref_reference_name_BB"><b><sup>B</sup></b></a></span>  raw unenriched long-grain white rice<br/>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_reference_name_CC"><a href="#ref_reference_name_CC"><b><sup>C</sup></b></a></span>  raw hard red winter wheat<br/>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_reference_name_DD"><a href="#ref_reference_name_DD"><b><sup>D</sup></b></a></span>  raw potato with flesh and skin<br/>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_reference_name_EE"><a href="#ref_reference_name_EE"><b><sup>E</sup></b></a></span>  raw cassava<br/>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_reference_name_FF"><a href="#ref_reference_name_FF"><b><sup>F</sup></b></a></span>  raw green soybeans<br/>
