</p>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potatoes_from_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2619" data-file-width="2267" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Potatoes_from_India.jpg/220px-Potatoes_from_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Potatoes_from_India.jpg/330px-Potatoes_from_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Potatoes_from_India.jpg/440px-Potatoes_from_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potatoes_from_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potatoes from North India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg/220px-Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg/330px-Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg/440px-Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_EarlyRose_sprouts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>'<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Rose_potato&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Early Rose potato (page does not exist)">Early Rose</a>' variety seed tuber with sprouts</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1328" data-file-width="1770" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG/220px-Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG/330px-Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG/440px-Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solanum_tuberosum_004.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Potato_fruit" title="Potato fruit">Potato fruit</a>, which is not edible</div></div></div>
<p>Potatoes contain <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxic</a> compounds known as <a href="/wiki/Glycoalkaloid" title="Glycoalkaloid">glycoalkaloids</a>, of which the most prevalent are <a href="/wiki/Solanine" title="Solanine">solanine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaconine" title="Chaconine">chaconine</a>. Solanine is found in other plants in the same family, <a href="/wiki/Solanaceae" title="Solanaceae">Solanaceae</a>, which includes such plants as deadly nightshade (<i><a href="/wiki/Atropa_belladonna" title="Atropa belladonna">Atropa belladonna</a></i>), henbane (<i><a href="/wiki/Hyoscyamus_niger" title="Hyoscyamus niger">Hyoscyamus niger</a></i>) and tobacco (<i><a href="/wiki/Nicotiana" title="Nicotiana">Nicotiana spp.</a></i>), as well as the food plants <a href="/wiki/Eggplant" title="Eggplant">eggplant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>. These compounds, which protect the potato plant from its predators, are generally concentrated in its leaves, flowers, sprouts, and fruits (in contrast to the tubers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In a summary of several studies, the glycoalkaloid content was highest in the flowers and sprouts and lowest in the tuber flesh. (The glycoalkaloid content was, in order from highest to lowest: flowers, sprouts, leaves, tuber skin, roots, berries, peel [skin plus outer cortex of tuber flesh], stems, and tuber flesh).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fried_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-fried-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to light, physical damage, and age increase glycoalkaloid content within the tuber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greening_of_potatoes_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greening_of_potatoes-71">[71]</a></sup> Cooking at high temperatures—over 170 °C (338 °F)—partly destroys these compounds. The concentration of glycoalkaloids in <a href="/wiki/Solanum_jamesii" title="Solanum jamesii">wild potatoes</a> is sufficient to produce toxic effects in humans. Glycoalkaloid poisoning may cause headaches, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cramps" title="Cramps">cramps</a>, and, in severe cases, coma and death. However, poisoning from cultivated potato varieties is very rare. Light exposure causes greening from <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> synthesis, giving a visual clue as to which areas of the tuber may have become more toxic. However, this does not provide a definitive guide, as greening and glycoalkaloid accumulation can occur independently of each other.
</p><p>Different potato varieties contain different levels of glycoalkaloids. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lenape_(potato)" title="Lenape (potato)">Lenape</a> variety was released in 1967 but was withdrawn in 1970 as it contained high levels of glycoalkaloids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boing_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-boing-72">[72]</a></sup> Since then, breeders developing new varieties test for this, and sometimes have to discard an otherwise promising <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>. Breeders try to keep glycoalkaloid levels below 200 mg/kg (0.0032 oz/lb) (200 <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">ppmw</a>). However, when these commercial varieties turn green, they can still approach <a href="/wiki/Solanine" title="Solanine">solanine</a> concentrations of 1,000 mg/kg (0.016 oz/lb) (1000 ppmw). In normal potatoes, analysis has shown solanine levels may be as little as 3.5% of the breeders' maximum, with 7–187 mg/kg (0.00011–0.00299 oz/lb) being found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> While a normal potato tuber has 12–20 mg/kg (0.00019–0.00032 oz/lb) of glycoalkaloid content, a green potato tuber contains 250–280 mg/kg (0.0040–0.0045 oz/lb) and its skin has 1,500–2,200 mg/kg (0.024–0.035 oz/lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Growth"></span><span class="anchor" id="Cultivation"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_cultivation">Growth and cultivation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_planting_machine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Potato_planting_machine.JPG/220px-Potato_planting_machine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Potato_planting_machine.JPG/330px-Potato_planting_machine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Potato_planting_machine.JPG/440px-Potato_planting_machine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_planting_machine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potato planting</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg/220px-Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg/330px-Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg/440px-Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tractors_in_Potato_Field.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potato field in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Fairfield,_Maine" title="Fort Fairfield, Maine">Fort Fairfield, Maine</a></div></div></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Seed_potato"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seed_potatoes">Seed potatoes</span></h3>
<p>Potatoes are generally grown from <i>seed potatoes</i>, tubers specifically grown to be free from disease and to provide consistent and healthy plants. To be disease free, the areas where seed potatoes are grown are selected with care. In the US, this restricts production of seed potatoes to only 15 states out of all 50 states where potatoes are grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> These locations are selected for their cold, hard winters that kill pests and summers with long sunshine hours for optimum growth. In the UK, most seed potatoes originate in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, in areas where westerly winds reduce <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphid</a> attack and the spread of <a href="/wiki/Potato_virus_Y" title="Potato virus Y">potato virus</a> <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Potatoes can also be grown from true seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggers_2021_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eggers_2021-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases_of_growth">Phases of growth</span></h3>
<p>Potato growth can be divided into five phases. During the first phase, sprouts emerge from the seed potatoes and root growth begins. During the second, <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> begins as the plant develops leaves and branches above-ground and <a href="/wiki/Stolon" title="Stolon">stolons</a> develop from lower leaf <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axil" title="Axil">axils</a> on the below-ground stem. In the third phase the tips of the stolons swell forming new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubers" title="Tubers">tubers</a> and the shoots continue to grow and flowers typically develop soon after. Tuber bulking occurs during the fourth phase, when the plant begins investing the majority of its resources in its newly formed tubers. At this phase, several factors are critical to a good yield: optimal <a href="/wiki/Soil_moisture" title="Soil moisture">soil moisture</a> and temperature, soil nutrient availability and balance, and resistance to <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pest attacks</a>. The fifth phase is the maturation of the tubers: the leaves and stems senesce and the tuber skins harden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JefferiesLawson1991_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-JefferiesLawson1991-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges">Challenges</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG/220px-Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG/330px-Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG/440px-Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_bag_cultivation.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potatoes grown in a tall bag are common in gardens as they minimize the amount of digging required at harvest</div></div></div><p>New tubers may start growing at the surface of the soil. Since exposure to light leads to an undesirable greening of the skins and the development of <a href="/wiki/Solanine" title="Solanine">solanine</a> as a protection from the sun's rays, growers cover surface tubers. Commercial growers cover them by piling additional soil around the base of the plant as it grows (called "hilling" up, or in British English "earthing up"). An alternative method, used by home gardeners and smaller-scale growers, involves covering the growing area with <a href="/wiki/Mulch" title="Mulch">mulches</a> such as straw or plastic sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cornell1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-cornell1-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Correct potato husbandry can be an arduous task in some circumstances. Good ground preparation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harrowing" title="Harrowing">harrowing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">plowing</a>, and rolling are always needed, along with a little grace from the weather and a good source of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Three successive plowings, with associated harrowing and rolling, are desirable before planting. Eliminating all root-weeds is desirable in potato cultivation. In general, the potatoes themselves are grown from the eyes of another potato and not from seed. Home gardeners often plant a piece of potato with two or three eyes in a hill of mounded soil. Commercial growers plant potatoes as a row crop using seed tubers, young plants or microtubers and may mound the entire row. Seed potato crops are <a href="/wiki/Roguing" title="Roguing">rogued</a> in some countries to eliminate diseased plants or those of a different variety from the seed crop.
</p><p>Potatoes are sensitive to heavy <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">frosts</a>, which damage them in the ground. Even cold weather makes potatoes more susceptible to bruising and possibly later rotting, which can quickly ruin a large stored crop.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_disease">Pests and disease</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_potato_diseases" title="List of potato diseases">List of potato diseases</a></div><p>
The historically significant <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">Phytophthora infestans</a></i> (late blight) remains an ongoing problem in Europe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlDis2011_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-PlDis2011-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Other potato diseases include <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizoctonia" title="Rhizoctonia">Rhizoctonia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sclerotinia" title="Sclerotinia">Sclerotinia</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Pectobacterium_carotovorum" title="Pectobacterium carotovorum">black leg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powdery_mildew" title="Powdery mildew">powdery mildew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powdery_scab" title="Powdery scab">powdery scab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potato_leafroll_virus" title="Potato leafroll virus">leafroll virus</a>.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2003" data-file-width="2790" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg/220px-Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg/330px-Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg/440px-Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phytophtora_infestans-effects.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A potato infected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_blight" title="Late blight">late blight</a></div></div></div>
<p>Insects that commonly transmit potato diseases or damage the plants include the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_potato_beetle" title="Colorado potato beetle">Colorado potato beetle</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potato_tuber_moth" title="Potato tuber moth">potato tuber moth</a>, the green peach aphid (<i><a href="/wiki/Myzus_persicae" title="Myzus persicae">Myzus persicae</a></i>), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potato_aphid" title="Potato aphid">potato aphid</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tuta_absoluta" title="Tuta absoluta">Tuta absoluta</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Beet_leafhopper" title="Beet leafhopper">beet leafhoppers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrips" title="Thrips">thrips</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mites" title="Mites">mites</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Potato_cyst_nematode" title="Potato cyst nematode">potato cyst nematode</a> is a microscopic worm that feeds on the roots, thus causing the potato plants to wilt. Since its eggs can survive in the soil for several years, <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> is recommended.
According to an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Working_Group" title="Environmental Working Group">Environmental Working Group</a> analysis of USDA and FDA pesticide residue tests performed from 2000 through 2008, 84% of the 2,216 tested potato samples contained detectable traces of at least one pesticide. A total of 36 unique pesticides were detected on potatoes over the 2,216 samples, though no individual sample contained more than 6 unique pesticide traces, and the average was 1.29 detectable unique pesticide traces per sample. The average quantity of all pesticide traces found in the 2,216 samples was 1.602 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>. While this was a very low value of pesticide residue, it was the highest amongst the 50 vegetables analyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvest">Harvest</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="767" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg/220px-AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg/330px-AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg/440px-AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AVR_Puma_4.0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A modern potato harvester</div></div></div>
<p>At harvest time, gardeners usually dig up potatoes with a long-handled, three-prong "grape" (or graip), i.e., a <a href="/wiki/Garden_fork" title="Garden fork">spading fork</a>, or a potato hook, which is similar to the graip but with tines at a 90<a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">°</a> angle to the handle. In larger plots, the plow is the fastest implement for unearthing potatoes. Commercial harvesting is typically done with large <a href="/wiki/Potato_harvester" title="Potato harvester">potato harvesters</a>, which scoop up the plant and surrounding earth. This is transported up an apron chain consisting of steel links several feet wide, which separates some of the dirt. The chain deposits into an area where further separation occurs. Different designs use different systems at this point. The most complex designs use vine choppers and shakers, along with a blower system to separate the potatoes from the plant. The result is then usually run past workers who continue to sort out plant material, stones, and rotten potatoes before the potatoes are continuously delivered to a wagon or truck. Further inspection and separation occurs when the potatoes are unloaded from the field vehicles and put into storage.
</p><p>Potatoes are usually cured after harvest to improve skin-set. Skin-set is the process by which the skin of the potato becomes resistant to skinning damage. Potato tubers may be susceptible to skinning at harvest and suffer skinning damage during harvest and handling operations. Curing allows the skin to fully set and any wounds to heal. Wound-healing prevents infection and water-loss from the tubers during storage. Curing is normally done at relatively warm temperatures (10 to 16 °C or 50 to 60 °F) with high humidity and good gas-exchange if at all possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_%281%29.jpg/220px-Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_%281%29.jpg/330px-Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_%281%29.jpg/440px-Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_transportation_to_cold_storage_in_India_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potato transportation to cold storage in India</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potato_farming_in_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3073" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Potato_farming_in_India.jpg/220px-Potato_farming_in_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Potato_farming_in_India.jpg/330px-Potato_farming_in_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Potato_farming_in_India.jpg/440px-Potato_farming_in_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potato_farming_in_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potato farming in India</div></div></div><p>Storage facilities need to be carefully designed to keep the potatoes alive and slow the natural process of sprouting which involves the breakdown of starch. It is crucial that the storage area be dark, ventilated well, and, for long-term storage, maintained at temperatures near 4 °C (39 °F). For short-term storage, temperatures of about 7 to 10 °C (45 to 50 °F) are preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crosstree_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-crosstree-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Temperatures below 4 °C (39 °F) convert the starch in potatoes into sugar, which alters their taste and cooking qualities and leads to higher <a href="/wiki/Acrylamide" title="Acrylamide">acrylamide</a> levels in the cooked product, especially in deep-fried dishes. The discovery of acrylamides in starchy foods in 2002 has led to international health concerns.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is not likely that the acrylamides in burnt or well-cooked food causes cancer in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cruk_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-cruk-11">[11]</a></sup>
Chemicals are used to suppress sprouting of tubers during storage. <a href="/wiki/Chlorpropham" title="Chlorpropham">Chlorpropham</a> (CIPC) is the main chemical used, but toxicity concerns have led to it being banned in the EU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epp_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-epp-86">[86]</a></sup> Alternatives are applying <a href="/wiki/Maleic_hydrazide" title="Maleic hydrazide">maleic hydrazide</a> to the crop whilst it is still growing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> or the use of <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>, spearmint and orange oils and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,4-dimethylnaphthalene" title="1,4-dimethylnaphthalene">1,4-dimethylnaphthalene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epp_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-epp-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under optimum conditions in commercial warehouses, potatoes can be stored for up to 10–12 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crosstree_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-crosstree-85">[85]</a></sup> The commercial storage and retrieval of potatoes involves several phases: first <i>drying</i> surface moisture; <i>wound healing</i> at 85% to 95% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a> and temperatures below 25 °C (77 °F); a staged <i>cooling phase</i>; a <i>holding</i> phase; and a <i>reconditioning</i> phase, during which the tubers are slowly warmed. <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">Mechanical ventilation</a> is used at various points during the process to prevent condensation and the accumulation of carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crosstree_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-crosstree-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yield">Yield</span></h3>
<p>The world dedicated 18.6 million hectares (46 million acres) to potato cultivation in 2010; the world average yield was 17.4 tonnes per hectare (7.8 short tons per acre). The United States was the most productive country, with a nationwide average yield of 44.3 tonnes per hectare (19.8 short tons per acre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yield2010_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-yield2010-88">[88]</a></sup> United Kingdom was a close second.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> farmers have demonstrated some of the best commercial yields in the world, ranging between 60 and 80 tonnes per hectare, some reporting yields of 88 tonnes of potatoes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a big gap among various countries between high and low yields, even with the same variety of potato. Average potato yields in developed economies ranges between 38 and 44 tonnes per hectare. China and India accounted for over a third of world's production in 2010, and had yields of 14.7 and 19.9 tonnes per hectare respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yield2010_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-yield2010-88">[88]</a></sup> The yield gap between farms in developing economies and developed economies represents an opportunity loss of over 400 million tonnes of potato, or an amount greater than 2010 world potato production. Potato crop yields are determined by factors such as the crop breed, seed age and quality, crop management practices and the plant environment. Improvements in one or more of these yield determinants, and a closure of the yield gap, can be a major boost to food supply and farmer incomes in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> yield of potatoes—about 95 gigajoules per hectare (9.2 million kilocalories per acre)—is higher than that of maize (78 GJ/ha or 7.5 million kcal/acre), rice (77 GJ/ha or 7.4 million kcal/acre), wheat (31 GJ/ha or 3 million kcal/acre), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybeans" title="Soybeans">soybeans</a> (29 GJ/ha or 2.8 million kcal/acre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_potatoes" title="Climate change and potatoes">Climate change and potatoes</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> is predicted to have significant effects on global potato production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supply_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-supply-95">[95]</a></sup> Like many crops, potatoes are likely to be affected by changes in atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, temperature and precipitation, as well as interactions between these factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supply_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-supply-95">[95]</a></sup> As well as affecting potatoes directly, climate change will also affect the distributions and populations of many potato diseases and pests.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_potato_dishes" title="List of potato dishes">List of potato dishes</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Potato_cooking" title="Potato cooking">Potato cooking</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PreparedPotatoes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="1787" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PreparedPotatoes.jpg/220px-PreparedPotatoes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PreparedPotatoes.jpg/330px-PreparedPotatoes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PreparedPotatoes.jpg/440px-PreparedPotatoes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PreparedPotatoes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various, typically <a href="/wiki/American_cuisine" title="American cuisine">American</a> potato preparations: (clockwise from top left) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potato_chips" title="Potato chips">potato chips</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hashbrowns" title="Hashbrowns">hashbrowns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tater_tots" title="Tater tots">tater tots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed potato</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Baked_potato" title="Baked potato">baked potato</a></div></div></div>
<p>Potatoes are prepared in many ways: skin-on or peeled, whole or cut up, with seasonings or without. The only requirement involves cooking to swell the starch granules. Most potato dishes are served hot but some are first cooked, then served cold, notably <a href="/wiki/Potato_salad" title="Potato salad">potato salad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potato_chip" title="Potato chip">potato chips (crisps)</a>. Common dishes are: <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed potatoes</a>, which are first boiled (usually peeled), and then mashed with milk or <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a> and butter; whole <a href="/wiki/Baked_potato" title="Baked potato">baked potatoes</a>; <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiled</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">steamed</a> potatoes; <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">French-fried potatoes or chips</a>; cut into cubes and <a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">roasted</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalloped_potatoes" title="Scalloped potatoes">scalloped</a>, diced, or sliced and fried (<a href="/wiki/Home_fries" title="Home fries">home fries</a>); grated into small thin strips and fried (<a href="/wiki/Hash_browns" title="Hash browns">hash browns</a>); grated and formed into <a href="/wiki/Dumpling" title="Dumpling">dumplings</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6sti" title="Rösti">Rösti</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potato_pancake" title="Potato pancake">potato pancakes</a>. Unlike many foods, potatoes can also be easily cooked in a <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave oven</a> and still retain nearly all of their nutritional value, provided they are covered in ventilated <a href="/wiki/Plastic_wrap" title="Plastic wrap">plastic wrap</a> to prevent moisture from escaping; this method produces a meal very similar to a steamed potato, while retaining the appearance of a conventionally baked potato. Potato chunks also commonly appear as a <a href="/wiki/Stew" title="Stew">stew</a> ingredient. Potatoes are boiled between 10 and 25<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> minutes, depending on size and type, to become soft.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_than_for_eating">Other than for eating</span></h3>
<p>Potatoes are also used for purposes other than eating by humans, for example:
</p>
<ul><li>Potatoes are used to brew alcoholic beverages such as <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poit%C3%ADn" title="Poitín">poitín</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Akvavit" title="Akvavit">akvavit</a>.</li>
<li>They are also used as <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> for <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>. Livestock-grade potatoes, considered too small and/or blemished to sell or market for human use but suitable for fodder use, have been called <i>chats</i> in some dialects. They may be stored in bins until use; they are sometimes <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">ensiled</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halliday_2015_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday_2015-97">[97]</a></sup> Some farmers prefer to steam them rather than feed them raw and are equipped to do so efficiently.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Potato_starch" title="Potato starch">Potato starch</a> is used in the food industry as a thickener and binder for soups and sauces, in the textile industry as an adhesive, and for the manufacturing of papers and boards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jai_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-jai-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>Potatoes are commonly used in plant research. The consistent parenchyma tissue, the clonal nature of the plant and the low metabolic activity make it an ideal <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model tissue</a> for experiments on wound-response studies and electron transport.</li>
<li>Potatoes have been delivered with personalized messages as a novelty. Potato delivery services include <a href="/wiki/Potato_Parcel" title="Potato Parcel">Potato Parcel</a> and Mail A Spud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_2016_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins_2016-100">[100]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Latin_America"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg/220px-Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg/330px-Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg/440px-Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peru_PapasRellenas2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Papa_rellena" title="Papa rellena">Papa rellena</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peruvian_cuisine" title="Peruvian cuisine">Peruvian cuisine</a> naturally contains the potato as a primary ingredient in many dishes, as around 3,000 varieties of this tuber are grown there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
Some of the more notable dishes include boiled potato as a base for several dishes or with <a href="/wiki/Aj%C3%AD_(sauce)" title="Ají (sauce)">ají</a>-based sauces like in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papa_a_la_Huanca%C3%ADna" title="Papa a la Huancaína">Papa a la Huancaína</a> or ocopa, diced potato for its use in soups like in cau cau, or in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carapulca" title="Carapulca">Carapulca</a> with dried potato (papa seca). Smashed condimented potato is used in causa Limeña and <a href="/wiki/Papa_rellena" title="Papa rellena">papa rellena</a>. French-fried potatoes are a typical ingredient in Peruvian stir-fries, including the classic dish <a href="/wiki/Lomo_saltado" title="Lomo saltado">lomo saltado</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chu%C3%B1o" title="Chuño">Chuño</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Freeze-drying" title="Freeze-drying">freeze-dried</a> potato product traditionally made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechuas" title="Quechuas">Quechua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aymara_people" title="Aymara people">Aymara</a> communities of <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and is known in various countries of South America, including <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, Bolivia, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>. In Chile's <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Archipelago" title="Chiloé Archipelago">Chiloé Archipelago</a>, potatoes are the main ingredient of many dishes, including milcaos, chapaleles, <a href="/wiki/Curanto" title="Curanto">curanto</a> and chochoca. In <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, the potato, as well as being a staple with most dishes, is featured in the hearty <i>locro de papas</i>, a thick soup of potato, squash, and cheese.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baked_Potato_(3662019664).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Baked_Potato_%283662019664%29.jpg/220px-Baked_Potato_%283662019664%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Baked_Potato_%283662019664%29.jpg/330px-Baked_Potato_%283662019664%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Baked_Potato_%283662019664%29.jpg/440px-Baked_Potato_%283662019664%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baked_Potato_(3662019664).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baked potato with sour cream and chives</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">UK</a>, potatoes form part of the traditional staple, <a href="/wiki/Fish_and_chips" title="Fish and chips">fish and chips</a>. Roast potatoes are commonly served as part of a <a href="/wiki/Sunday_roast" title="Sunday roast">Sunday roast dinner</a> and mashed potatoes form a major component of several other traditional dishes, such as <a href="/wiki/Shepherd%27s_pie" title="Shepherd's pie">shepherd's pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bubble_and_squeak" title="Bubble and squeak">bubble and squeak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangers_and_mash" title="Bangers and mash">bangers and mash</a>. New potatoes may be cooked with <a href="/wiki/Mentha" title="Mentha">mint</a> and are often served with butter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tattie_scone" title="Tattie scone">Tattie scone</a> is a popular Scottish dish containing potatoes. <a href="/wiki/Colcannon" title="Colcannon">Colcannon</a> is a traditional Irish food made with mashed potato, shredded <a href="/wiki/Kale" title="Kale">kale</a> or cabbage, and onion; <a href="/wiki/Champ_(food)" title="Champ (food)">champ</a> is a similar dish. <a href="/wiki/Boxty" title="Boxty">Boxty</a> pancakes are eaten throughout Ireland, although associated especially with the North, and in Irish diaspora communities; they are traditionally made with grated potatoes, soaked to loosen the starch and mixed with flour, buttermilk and baking powder. A variant eaten and sold in <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, is made with cooked and mashed potatoes.
</p><p>In the UK, <a href="/wiki/Game_chips" title="Game chips">game chips</a> are a traditional accompaniment to roast <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamebird" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pheasant" title="Pheasant">pheasant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partridge" title="Partridge">partridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quail" title="Quail">quail</a>.
</p><p>Powdered cooked potato has been sold in the UK since the 1960s as <a href="/wiki/Smash_(instant_mashed_potato)" title="Smash (instant mashed potato)">Smash</a> and is used as a food for camping <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and domestically.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Halu%C5%A1ky" title="Halušky">Halushky</a> are the national dish of many Slavic nations. Halušky dumplings are made from a batter consisting of flour and grated potatoes. <i><a href="/wiki/Bryndzov%C3%A9_halu%C5%A1ky" title="Bryndzové halušky">Bryndzové halušky</a></i> are associated to <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovak</a> cuisine in particular.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="1016" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg/220px-Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg/330px-Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg/440px-Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German <i>Bauernfrühstück</i> ("farmer's breakfast")</div></div></div>
<p>In Germany, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern</a> (Finland, Latvia and especially <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian countries</a>), Eastern Europe (Russia, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>) and Poland, newly harvested, early ripening varieties are considered a special delicacy. Boiled whole and served un-peeled with <a href="/wiki/Dill" title="Dill">dill</a>, these "new potatoes" are traditionally consumed with <a href="/wiki/Pickled_herring" title="Pickled herring">Baltic herring</a>. Puddings made from grated potatoes (<a href="/wiki/Kugel" title="Kugel">kugel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kugelis" title="Kugelis">kugelis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potato_babka" title="Potato babka">potato babka</a>) are popular items of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_cuisine" title="Ashkenazi cuisine">Ashkenazi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_cuisine" title="Lithuanian cuisine">Lithuanian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_cuisine" title="Belarusian cuisine">Belarusian</a> cuisine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bremzen90_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bremzen90-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/German_fries" title="German fries">German fried potatoes</a> and various versions of Potato salad are part of <a href="/wiki/German_cuisine" title="German cuisine">German cuisine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bauernfr%C3%BChst%C3%BCck" title="Bauernfrühstück">Bauernfrühstück</a> (literally <i>farmer's breakfast</i>) is a warm German dish made from fried potatoes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a>, ham and vegetables.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cepelinai_2,_Vilnius,_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1978" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cepelinai_2%2C_Vilnius%2C_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg/220px-Cepelinai_2%2C_Vilnius%2C_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cepelinai_2%2C_Vilnius%2C_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg/330px-Cepelinai_2%2C_Vilnius%2C_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cepelinai_2%2C_Vilnius%2C_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg/440px-Cepelinai_2%2C_Vilnius%2C_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cepelinai_2,_Vilnius,_Lithuania_-_Diliff.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cepelinai" title="Cepelinai">Cepelinai</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cepelinai" title="Cepelinai">Cepelinai</a> is the national dish of <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>. They are a type of <a href="/wiki/Dumpling" title="Dumpling">dumpling</a> made from grated raw potatoes boiled in water and usually stuffed with <a href="/wiki/Ground_meat" title="Ground meat">minced meat</a>, although sometimes dry cottage cheese (<a href="/wiki/Curd" title="Curd">curd</a>) or mushrooms are used instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Western Europe, especially in Belgium, sliced potatoes are fried to create <i>frieten</i>, the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_fried_potatoes" title="French fried potatoes">French fried potatoes</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Stamppot" title="Stamppot">Stamppot</a></i>, a traditional Dutch meal, is based on mashed potatoes mixed with vegetables.
</p><p>In France, the most notable potato dish is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hachis_Parmentier" title="Hachis Parmentier">Hachis Parmentier</a></i>, named after <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Augustin_Parmentier" title="Antoine-Augustin Parmentier">Antoine-Augustin Parmentier</a>, a French pharmacist, nutritionist, and agronomist who, in the late 18th century, was instrumental in the acceptance of the potato as an edible crop in the country. <i><a href="/wiki/P%C3%A2t%C3%A9_aux_pommes_de_terre" title="Pâté aux pommes de terre">Pâté aux pommes de terre</a></i> is a regional potato dish from the central <a href="/wiki/Allier" title="Allier">Allier</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limousin_(region)" title="Limousin (region)">Limousin</a> regions. <i><a href="/wiki/Gratin_dauphinois" title="Gratin dauphinois">Gratin dauphinois</a></i>, consisting of baked thinly sliced potatoes with cream or milk, and <i><a href="/wiki/Tartiflette" title="Tartiflette">tartiflette</a></i>, with Reblochon cheese, are also widespread.
</p><p>In the north of Italy, in particular, in the <a href="/wiki/Friuli" title="Friuli">Friuli</a> region of the northeast, potatoes serve to make a type of pasta called <a href="/wiki/Gnocchi" title="Gnocchi">gnocchi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Similarly, cooked and mashed potatoes or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potato_flour" title="Potato flour">potato flour</a> can be used in the <a href="/wiki/Kn%C3%B6del" title="Knödel">Knödel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dumpling" title="Dumpling">dumpling</a> eaten with or added to meat dishes all over central and Eastern Europe, but especially in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>. Potatoes form one of the main ingredients in many soups such as the <a href="/wiki/Vichyssoise" title="Vichyssoise">vichyssoise</a> and Albanian potato and cabbage soup. In western Norway, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komle" title="Komle">komle</a> is popular.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Potato_pancake" title="Potato pancake">Potato pancakes</a> are popular all over Central Europe, and are also known in Scandinavia, and in <a href="/wiki/Jewish_cuisine" title="Jewish cuisine">Jewish cuisine</a>.
</p><p>A traditional <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> dish is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canarian_wrinkly_potatoes" title="Canarian wrinkly potatoes">Canarian wrinkly potatoes</a> or <i>papas arrugadas</i>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tortilla_de_patatas" title="Tortilla de patatas">Tortilla de patatas</a></i> (potato omelette) and <i><a href="/wiki/Patatas_bravas" title="Patatas bravas">patatas bravas</a></i> (a dish of fried potatoes in a spicy tomato sauce) are near-universal constituent of Spanish <a href="/wiki/Tapas" title="Tapas">tapas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h3>
<p>In the US, potatoes have become one of the most widely consumed crops and thus have a variety of preparation methods and condiments. <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">French fries</a> and often <a href="/wiki/Hash_browns" title="Hash browns">hash browns</a> are commonly found in typical American fast-food burger "joints" and cafeterias. One popular favourite involves a baked potato with cheddar cheese (or sour cream and <a href="/wiki/Chives" title="Chives">chives</a>) on top, and in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> "smashed potatoes" (a chunkier variation on mashed potatoes, retaining the peel) have a great popularity. Potato flakes are popular as an instant variety of mashed potatoes, which reconstitute into mashed potatoes by adding water, with butter or oil and salt to taste. A regional dish of <a href="/wiki/Central_New_York" title="Central New York">Central New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salt_potatoes" title="Salt potatoes">salt potatoes</a> are bite-size new potatoes boiled in water saturated with salt then served with melted butter. At more formal dinners, a common practice includes taking small red potatoes, slicing them, and roasting them in an iron skillet. Among <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">American Jews</a>, the practice of eating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latkes" title="Latkes">latkes</a> (fried potato pancakes) is common during the festival of <a href="/wiki/Hanukkah" title="Hanukkah">Hanukkah</a>.
</p><p>A traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acadian" title="Acadian">Acadian</a> dish from <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> is known as <i>poutine râpée</i>. The Acadian poutine is a ball of grated and <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed potato</a>, salted, sometimes filled with pork in the centre, and boiled. The result is a moist ball about the size of a <a href="/wiki/Baseball_(ball)" title="Baseball (ball)">baseball</a>. It is commonly eaten with salt and pepper or <a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">brown sugar</a>. It is believed to have originated from the German <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kl%C3%B6%C3%9Fe" title="Klöße">Klöße</a></i>, prepared by early German settlers who lived among the Acadians. <i><a href="/wiki/Poutine" title="Poutine">Poutine</a></i>, by contrast, is a hearty serving of French fries, fresh <a href="/wiki/Cheese_curd" title="Cheese curd">cheese curds</a> and hot gravy. Tracing its origins to <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> in the 1950s, it has become a widespread and popular dish throughout Canada.
</p><p>Potato grading for <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> potatoes is performed in which No. 1 potatoes are the highest quality and No. 2 are rated as lower in quality due to their appearance (e.g. blemishes or bruises, pointy ends).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Potato density assessment can be performed by floating them in brines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sivasankar_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sivasankar-109">[109]</a></sup> High-density potatoes are desirable in the production of dehydrated mashed potatoes, potato crisps and french fries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sivasankar_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sivasankar-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, the potato is a very popular traditional staple. In India, the most popular potato dishes are <i>aloo ki sabzi</i>, <a href="/wiki/Batata_vada" title="Batata vada">batata vada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Samosa" title="Samosa">samosa</a>, which is spicy mashed potato mixed with a small amount of vegetable stuffed in conical dough, and deep fried. Potatoes are also a major ingredient as fast food items, such as aloo chaat, where they are deep fried and served with <a href="/wiki/Chutney" title="Chutney">chutney</a>. In Northern India, alu dum and alu paratha are a favourite part of the diet; the first is a spicy curry of boiled potato, the second is a type of stuffed chapati.
</p><p>A dish called <a href="/wiki/Masala_dosa" title="Masala dosa">masala dosa</a> from South India is very notable all over India. It is a thin pancake of rice and <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">pulse</a> batter rolled over spicy smashed potato and eaten with sambhar and chutney. Poori in south India in particular in Tamil Nadu is almost always taken with smashed potato masal. Other favourite dishes are alu tikki and pakoda items.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vada_pav" title="Vada pav">Vada pav</a> is a popular vegetarian fast food dish in Mumbai and other regions in the Maharashtra in India.
</p><p>Aloo posto (a curry with potatoes and poppy seeds) is immensely popular in East India, especially Bengal. Although potatoes are not native to India, it has become a vital part of food all over the country especially North Indian food preparations. In Tamil Nadu this tuber acquired a name based on its appearance 'urulai-k-kizhangu' (உருளைக் கிழங்கு) meaning cylindrical tuber.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Aloo_gosht" title="Aloo gosht">Aloo gosht</a>, Potato and meat <a href="/wiki/Curry" title="Curry">curry</a>, is one of the popular dishes in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span></h3>
<p>In East Asia, particularly Southeast Asia, rice is by far the predominant starch crop, with potatoes a secondary crop, especially in China and Japan. However, it is used in northern China where rice is not easily grown, with a popular dish being <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">青椒土豆丝</span></span> (<i>qīng jiāo tǔ dòu sī</i>), made with green pepper, vinegar and thin slices of potato. In the winter, roadside sellers in northern China will also sell roasted potatoes. It is also occasionally seen in Korean and Thai cuisines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solomon_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_art">In art</span></h3>
<p>The potato has been an essential crop in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> Era. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> culture from Northern <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> made ceramics from the earth, water, and fire. This pottery was a sacred substance, formed in significant shapes and used to represent important themes. Potatoes are represented anthropomorphically as well as naturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the late 19th century, numerous images of potato harvesting appeared in European art, including the works of <a href="/wiki/Willem_Witsen" title="Willem Witsen">Willem Witsen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anton_Mauve" title="Anton Mauve">Anton Mauve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Gogh" title="Van Gogh">Van Gogh</a>'s 1885 painting <i><a href="/wiki/The_Potato_Eaters" title="The Potato Eaters">The Potato Eaters</a></i> portrays a family eating potatoes. Van Gogh said he wanted to depict peasants as they really were. He deliberately chose coarse and ugly models, thinking that they would be natural and unspoiled in his finished work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vgg_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-vgg-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet" title="Jean-François Millet">Jean-François Millet</a>'s <i>The Potato Harvest</i> depicts peasants working in the plains between Barbizon and Chailly. It presents a theme representative of the peasants' struggle for survival. Millet's technique for this work incorporated paste-like pigments thickly applied over a coarsely textured canvas.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:24px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg" title="The Potato Eaters by Van Gogh, 1885 (Van Gogh Museum)"><img alt="The Potato Eaters by Van Gogh, 1885 (Van Gogh Museum)" data-file-height="2517" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg/200px-Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg/300px-Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg/400px-Van-willem-vincent-gogh-die-kartoffelesser-03850.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg" title="The Potato Harvest by Jean-François Millet, 1855 (Walters Art Museum)"><img alt="The Potato Harvest by Jean-François Millet, 1855 (Walters Art Museum)" data-file-height="1486" data-file-width="1799" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg/194px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg/291px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg/388px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_-_The_Potato_Harvest_-_Walters_37115.jpg 2x" width="194"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Potato_Harvest" title="The Potato Harvest">The Potato Harvest</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet" title="Jean-François Millet">Jean-François Millet</a>, 1855 (<a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a>)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<p>Invented in 1949, and marketed and sold commercially by <a href="/wiki/Hasbro" title="Hasbro">Hasbro</a> in 1952, <a href="/wiki/Mr._Potato_Head" title="Mr. Potato Head">Mr. Potato Head</a> is an American toy that consists of a plastic potato and attachable plastic parts, such as ears and eyes, to make a face. It was the first toy ever advertised on television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VAC_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-VAC-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June, 1992 at the Muñoz Rivera Elementary School spelling bee in Trenton, New Jersey, U.S. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Dan_Quayle#Potatoe" title="Dan Quayle">Dan Quayle</a> was handed a flash card that incorrectly spelled "potato" as "potatoe" and then prompted a 12-year-old student to change his correct spelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> This incident was the subject of widespread ridicule.
</p>
